<div id="review_331852238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331852238" />
    <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers"><img alt="TheBookSmugglers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316717753p2/1223249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331852238?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TheBookSmugglers" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers">TheBookSmugglers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331852238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11012244678019699954">Originally reviewed on <a href='http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/05/book-review-blackout-by-mira-grant.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Smugglers</a><br/><br/><strong>**WARNING: THIS REVIEW CONTAINS UNAVOIDABLE SPOILERS FOR <em>FEED</em> AND <em>DEADLINE</em>. If you have not read the first two books in the trilogy and want to remain unspoiled, look away! YOU HAVE BEEN WARNED.**</strong><br/><br/>At the conclusion of <em>Deadline</em>, there have been some rather drastic revelations: Shaun is immune to Kellis-Amberlee, Georgia has been cloned by the CDC (oh, yeah, and the adopted brother and sister have had a longstanding sexual relationship). <em>Blackout</em> opens with </span>
  <span id="freeText11012244678019699954" style="display:none">Originally reviewed on <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/05/book-review-blackout-by-mira-grant.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Smugglers</a><br /><br /><strong>**WARNING: THIS REVIEW CONTAINS UNAVOIDABLE SPOILERS FOR <em>FEED</em> AND <em>DEADLINE</em>. If you have not read the first two books in the trilogy and want to remain unspoiled, look away! YOU HAVE BEEN WARNED.**</strong><br /><br />At the conclusion of <em>Deadline</em>, there have been some rather drastic revelations: Shaun is immune to Kellis-Amberlee, Georgia has been cloned by the CDC (oh, yeah, and the adopted brother and sister have had a longstanding sexual relationship). <em>Blackout</em> opens with a bang, much in the same way as its predecessor. Georgia finds herself an unwitting patient of the CDC, and while she knows immediately that she must be a clone (given the memory of her death and the fact that she no longer has retinal KA), she doesn't know <i>why</i> she has been cloned or what the CDC's endgame is. All she knows is that it must have to do with her brother, and it must have to do with a truth that someone very desperately wants to keep covered up. Not so far away, Shaun and the After the End Times team (Mahir, Alaric, Mags, and Becks) are dispatched on a crazy dangerous mission - Kellis-Amberlee has mutated and now can be carried by insect vectors, and after the last tropical storm deadly zombie-making mosquitoes are now on the large and the entire state of Florida is nanoseconds away from being declared officially lost. Shaun - still mourning for his lost sister and hanging onto sanity by a mere thread - and the crew must save Alaric's sister, capture a live mosquito sample for testing, and figure out why the virus mutated - be the cause natural, or manmade. <br /><br />Alternating points-of-view between Shaun's narrative and Georgia's, <em>Blackout</em> chronicles the last chapter of the Newsflesh trilogy as the Masons paths collide and together they fight to rip the lid off of a conspiracy so huge, it will rock the foundation of the post-Rising world. <br /><br />I am kind of at a loss when it comes to <i>Blackout</i>. I *loved* <i>Feed</i>. I loved the heavy exposition, the fascinating medical procedural tied to the political thriller. I loved Georgia's frank narration, and I loved how honest and forthright she was throughout. I loved this vision of a post-apocalyptic, zombie-filled world, and the steps humanity has taken to adapt, survive, and to rise. <br /><br />Needless to say, when I got through <em>Deadline</em>, I was a little less enthused. I still loved the world building and the underlying main storyline, but so many of the things I was so enamoured with in the first book were absent in the second. Most glaringly, Shaun is not half the narrator his sister was. There was also a ton of repetition (not just of pointless story exposition that leads nowhere, but also of key phrases - Shaun drinking a coke, muttering to himself/Georgia's ghost, grinning like a maniac and wanting to punch people in the face, etc) that detracted from the overall efficacy of the story. The political and medical thriller, the underlying conspiracy, is pushed to the backburner in favor of Shaun's (very quickly tiresome) glib narrative as he grapples with grief. <br /><br />In <em>Blackout</em>, I wanted so desperately for the book to return to the series' <i>Feed</i> roots, but alas. <i>Blackout</i> is better than <i>Deadline</i>, but failed to wow, shock or awe. I liked the alternating narrator conceit, tying the first two books together nicely in an attempt to bring both Georgia and Shaun back together again. That said, I found myself wanting to skip Shaun's narrative entirely - as to me it felt largely pointless and filled with the same tedious repetition I had to slog through in <em>Deadline</em>. The good news is that Georgia's narrative is as wonderful as I remembered from <i>Feed</i> but this time is rife with more internal struggle as she fights to form and understand a sense of self and identity within her new flesh, whilst simultaneously fighting and exposing the Umbrella Corporation-esque corruption of the CDC, and finding a way to escape and get back to her brother. As far as narrators go, Georgia remains one of the coolest, smartest, most capable protagonists I have had the pleasure of reading in a good long while. With regard to the other characters, my main complaint is how similar the majority of the characters sound to each other. Most everyone is a smarmy, fast-talking wiseguy with a mile-wide melodramatic streak, from the doctors to the Newsies. I like the additions to the cast this go-around, but the lack of distinct voices makes for a monotonous reading experience. [1. What's that old adage? When everyone's a wisecracking snarkist, <i>no one is</i>.] <br /><br />On the story and actual writing front, <em>Blackout</em> also leaves a girl wanting more. Well, actually, wanting <i>less</i>. The biggest issue with <em>Blackout</em> is its unnecessary length - the underlying conspiracy that runs through the trilogy, the truth that Georgia, Shaun and the gang are fighting so desperately to unveil? WE'VE KNOWN ABOUT IT SINCE BOOK 1 (and the beginning portion of book 2)! There is absolutely no need for the book to be half as long as it is, chock-full of repetitive action, driving scenes, medical tests, and so on that have no baring on the actual progression of the story or development of the characters.[2. Especially coming off reading such a fantastically taught and expertly written short novel in <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/05/book-review-after-the-fall-before-the-fall-during-the-fall-by-nancy-kress.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Nancy Kress's <em>After the Fall, Before the Fall, During the Fall</em></a>, <em>Blackout</em>'s many excesses were all the more glaring!] Even the little epigraphs preceding each chapter - and I shouldn't call them "little" because there are at least 2 each time, and usually span at least a paragraph a pop - became tiresome and repetitive.[3. On a side note, that's a shame because some of the epigraphs were actually pertinent and reveal more to the story at large - but because 90% of them are pointless filler, the tendency is to want to skip them altogether.] That said, the actual conspiracy itself is a fantastic twist (well, not so twisty since we've kind of known about it for a while), and once the action and story proper actually starts moving along, <em>Blackout</em> becomes a much more enjoyable read. <br /><br />I can't write this review without addressing the two other significant detractors for me, personally, though. These are the two huge Jump the Shark moments from <em>Blackout</em>: 1. The Relationship between Georgia and Shaun; and 2. The Cloning/Pseudoscience/Shaun's Immunity Revelations. First, regarding the relationship between brother and sister, I simply cannot buy it. Not even in this book, not even with Georgia's 'explanation' (which feels very much like an editorial response to criticism of book 2 and that revelation). I don't care if the nature of their relationship is something that Georgia and Shaun <i>never wrote down</i> - the fact that we are living inside both Georgia and Shaun's heads for the full trilogy means that at some point, in <i>Feed</i>, Georgia could have/should have made some sort of reference to her very intimate, soulmate bond with her non-biological brother. I simply do not buy it (your mileage may vary, of course, but to me this revelation and attempt at rationalization felt inauthentic). <br /><br />Regarding the second, Shaun's immunity to KA and Georgia's cloning are also 'explained', and while these explanations are within the realm of possibility (this IS a zombie novel, after all), I still can't help but feel a little, well, unhappy with the way things turn out. The reason why <i>Feed</i> was such a powerful, resonant novel is because of its grounding in more tangible science, its taut political relevance, and the medical thriller aspect to the book. We lose that in <em>Deadline</em> and <em>Blackout</em>, which turns to fringe scifi with neural/synapse photography/memory imprinting and cloning of a fully grown human (still not sure how that worked so quickly). Mira Grant does a phenomenal job with making these applied phlebotinum technologies and sciences work, but it's a far cry from the more sturdy applications in <i>Feed</i> (again, your mileage may vary).<br /><br />All these criticisms voiced, I still finished <em>Blackout</em> and enjoyed the experience, for both the novel and for the series as a whole. The Newsflesh books have tremendous crossover genre potential - I hesitate to label them zombie books because the zombies play such a tangential, minor role to the characters and the true villains of the piece (not to mention the virus itself). While I wasn't wholly satisfied with the way things turned out, and <em>Feed</em> is clearly the vastly superior novel of the trilogy, <em>Blackout</em> is a solid read. And if you've come this far in the trilogy, you're gonna have to finish it. Right? Recommended...albeit with reservations.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11012244678019699954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170511033?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13022715-blackout">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=dystopian-post-apocalyptic">dystopian-post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=arcs-netgalley">arcs-netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=owned-book">owned-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5136858-bonnie?shelf=zombies-like-brains">zombies-like-brains</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170511033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10449538762674000663"><i>'This is as close as we could get to an ending. The world goes on. Zombies or no zombies, political conspiracy or no political conspiracy, the world goes on.<br/>I think I like it that way.'</i><br/><br/>Ah, the end is finally here. Blackout: the highly anticipated final book in the Newsflesh trilogy. For those of you that have yet to read this series feel free to continue - no spoilers!<br/><br/>When I first heard about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7094569.Feed__Newsflesh_Trilogy___1_' title='Feed (Newsflesh Trilogy, #1) by Mira Grant' rel='nofollow noopener'>Feed</a>, I dismissed it immediately when 'political intrigue' and 'zombies' were put into the same descript</span>
  <span id="freeText10449538762674000663" style="display:none"><i>'This is as close as we could get to an ending. The world goes on. Zombies or no zombies, political conspiracy or no political conspiracy, the world goes on.<br />I think I like it that way.'</i><br /><br />Ah, the end is finally here. Blackout: the highly anticipated final book in the Newsflesh trilogy. For those of you that have yet to read this series feel free to continue - no spoilers!<br /><br />When I first heard about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7094569.Feed__Newsflesh_Trilogy___1_" title="Feed (Newsflesh Trilogy, #1) by Mira Grant" rel="nofollow noopener">Feed</a>, I dismissed it immediately when 'political intrigue' and 'zombies' were put into the same descriptive sentence. I mean, really? How on earth could that possibly work without some serious eye-rolling? Well, Mira/Seanan managed to make it work and it was <u>amazing</u>. Seriously, these are the most well-written books I have ever had the pleasure of reading. Exciting, intense, emotional and positively sensational.<br /><br />The single most thing that I love about this entire series is the obvious amount of research that was put into these. There will be pages where the book goes into detail about viruses and immunity and spontaneous amplification and I'll think I'm reading some sort of textbook... the most awesome textbook in existence. In the Acknowledgment section she thanks medical professional (human and animals), gun experts and even epidemiologists (medical scientists that study in the transmission and control of diseases - thanks Google!) that assisted her with the necessary research for these novels. That extreme detail makes this hands down the most believable zombie book/series in existence. <br /><br />I could go on for days about my love for this series but I'm going to restrain myself. I'm extremely sad that the series is over yet at the same time I'm extremely pleased with how well all the loose ends were tied up. The end was less than perfect but more than appropriate. I think fans of this series will be pleased. <br /><br />Mira/Seanan? Thank you. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_202469426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202469426" />
    <a title="Regina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3104032-regina"><img alt="Regina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362864551p2/3104032.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202469426?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Regina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Regina" href="/user/show/3104032-regina">Regina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104032-regina?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104032-regina?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3104032-regina?shelf=favorite-authors">favorite-authors</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202469426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15222179678990070130">This was fanflippingtastic! The best in the series. I just finished and I am shaking. I don't think there will be anything this good for me, ever again. This is not a review, lol, I will be back to write a more coherent and better put together thoughts. Mira Grant is a brilliant writer.<br/><br/>**<br/>I was in love with this book from the beginning. I should say upfront, I liked Feed but not as much as Deadline, which I loved and not as much as Blackout, which is my favorite. For me, Mira really hit her strid</span>
  <span id="freeText15222179678990070130" style="display:none">This was fanflippingtastic! The best in the series. I just finished and I am shaking. I don't think there will be anything this good for me, ever again. This is not a review, lol, I will be back to write a more coherent and better put together thoughts. Mira Grant is a brilliant writer.<br /><br />**<br />I was in love with this book from the beginning. I should say upfront, I liked Feed but not as much as Deadline, which I loved and not as much as Blackout, which is my favorite. For me, Mira really hit her stride with Blackout. The alternating points of view were very effective and I loved the blog posts from the various team members. The way Mira tells her story, both in first person point of view from various characters and in a journal format via quotes and blog excerpts, really worked for me. It gave the story a multi-layered feel.<br /> <br />Blackout was non-stop struggle, fight and chase from the beginning. The action never stopped but it was my favorite kind of action. I skim or close my eyes during fight scenes and car chase sequences but the action in Blackout had me hooked. I did not miss a word. I wondered about people’s motives, I worried that there would be unresolved issues and I worried about my favorite characters. I shouldn’t have worried. Not everyone can live or survive in a world like this one, but the characters are dealt with fairly and I was satisfied with the outcome.<br /> <br />A book about a zombie plague that affected the world’s mammal population and a future dytsopia setting is bound to have its unbelievable moments, but for me this never happened. Mira Grant writes this book in such a way that it is believable. A lingering question at the end of every zombie book is –how did the zombie plague happen? Well, in Newsflesh Mira Grant lays it out for the readers. We know why it happened. She provides enough detail to readers so that the science is acceptable. Her world building is not just in boundaries and political alignments, but is is also done with science. <br /><br />Political plots can be a real yawner. Ther are some books with political plotlines that I liked (Kushiel’s Dart and Game of Thrones are some examples), but I prefer an action based plot or a character driven plot — Blackout manages to have both and has politics intertwined and somehow is still. There is political intrigue, backstabbing and power hungry grabbers. But there are other political elements as well. One of the political themes that I picked up on, is the idea that citizens should not trust a government or authority that derives its power from fear. Mira touches on this theme very subtly and effectively but does not hit the reader over the head with it. But what I thought was interesting, but questioning the government on the issue of fear and safety, she is calling into question the premise of the world she has constructed. Are those multiple blood tests really necessary? Necessary or not, they play a key role in the books but there is a hint that these blood tests may be used as a method of pacification and mollification rather than simply a safety measure.<br /> <br />While reading the first two books I often wondered — what about the people who live off the grid? Was that even possible? Mira takes the readers off the grid in Deadline; we get to see people who have walked away from the fences and the government protection. While the first two books had more of a dystopia setting feel by showing us the off-the grid folks it was evident that the apocalypse was still going on. And that is downright scary. It really isn’t under control but what the government is doing to maintain the problem isn’t working. There are so many issues addressed in this book — power, greed, love, desparation …..<br /> <br />Ugh, I am so sad this trilogy is over. I would love to have more stories about these characters. I am hoping Mira Grant considers returning to this world. She has created something so unique in the zombie genre that deserves to be revisited.<br /><br />To read an interview with Mira Grant and for more of this review and others, check out <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.badassbookreviews.com">www.badassbookreviews.com</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15222179678990070130" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2876598245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2876598245" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2876598245?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/14760846-blackout">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2876598245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer929401864249564292">This is the conclusion to the trilogy and it went out with a bang just like I had hoped.<br/><br/>Georgia has been cloned and that clone was waking up right at the end of the previous book. The beginning of this one has her trying to figure out what is going on, where she is and if she can trust any of the people around her. <br/>Meanwhile, Shaun is spiralling out of control more and more but at least he's got a plan on what to do before it's too late for him. <br/>The problem? The government, which is to say the C</span>
  <span id="freeText929401864249564292" style="display:none">This is the conclusion to the trilogy and it went out with a bang just like I had hoped.<br /><br />Georgia has been cloned and that clone was waking up right at the end of the previous book. The beginning of this one has her trying to figure out what is going on, where she is and if she can trust any of the people around her. <br />Meanwhile, Shaun is spiralling out of control more and more but at least he's got a plan on what to do before it's too late for him. <br />The problem? The government, which is to say the CDC, has ramped up their game as well.<br />Thus, the reader finally sees more of the true scale of this conspiracy and learns about what is scientifically possible in this near future.<br /><br />Almost unexpectedly, I was actually touched and looking forward to <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[seeing Georgia and Shaun finally meeting up again <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. True, the whole plot point felt a liiiiiittle like a cop-out so I remained suspicious and I'm still not sure it was a good idea to have <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Georgia and Shaun be secret lovers all the while because most people growing up together aren't interested in one another that way <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but the characters were quite likeable so I have no trouble admitting I had a little "aaawww" moment.<br /><br />We continue to follow people for who news isn't just a way of getting more readers or better ratings, but who take the truth seriously and believe in accountability. So we are talking about idealists. Nevertheless, it was done in a pretty good way and used to highlight all the things power can corrupt. As such, the set-up still worked. However, it wouldn't have worked this well without the science (though <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the cloning taking care of the MC's "defect" wasn't actually a good message for me personally <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) and action, the break-ins and break-outs as well as all kinds of twisted animals (both zombie bears and gigantic mosquitos). The reunion with characters we've known since book 1 but lost touch with, was nice as well, bringing it all together the way it should.<br /><br />It was heartbreaking to see the world so many years after the zombie apocalypse and to see that it could actually get worse. That it was realistic to all go so wrong, in fact. Many of the themes here are, of course, real-life problems we have already created for ourselves. Like practically being addicted to being afraid. Like people being addicted to being the firsts to report about something and facts be damned. Like most people only reading headlines. Like the news (no matter from which side of the spectrum) trying to influence and even steer events. Like the road to hell being paved with good intentions. Like protection turning into imprisonment. <br />Just as heartbreaking was the revelation right at the end about the virus and <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Emily having been replaced by a clone so the real Emily could be used to keep the President under the CDC's control - which makes you wonder how many clones are roaming this Earth <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />A truly great series, highly atmospheric and with a fresh look at an old(er) genre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="929401864249564292" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_225104743" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225104743" />
    <a title="Isamlq" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4016030-isamlq"><img alt="Isamlq" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483590305p2/4016030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225104743?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Isamlq" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Isamlq" href="/user/show/4016030-isamlq">Isamlq</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4016030-isamlq?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4016030-isamlq?shelf=aaw-yeah">aaw-yeah</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4016030-isamlq?shelf=2012-favorites">2012-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225104743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17166662644870441601"><b>This book!</b> <br/><br/>No, seriously, <i>this book…! </i> I’d sworn to take it slow and sure I was keeping to said goal, but once I reached the 20% mark, well there was no looking back for me. I didn’t even bother to hide the fact that I was reading this at work. So picture me thus: nose buried in kindle, more often than not, with palm over mouth to keep from screaming. <i>It was that good.</i> <br/><br/><i>Details.</i> I can only imagine the research that must have gone into this one. I am very impressed with how she put in all those de</span>
  <span id="freeText17166662644870441601" style="display:none"><b>This book!</b> <br /><br />No, seriously, <i>this book…! </i> I’d sworn to take it slow and sure I was keeping to said goal, but once I reached the 20% mark, well there was no looking back for me. I didn’t even bother to hide the fact that I was reading this at work. So picture me thus: nose buried in kindle, more often than not, with palm over mouth to keep from screaming. <i>It was that good.</i> <br /><br /><i>Details.</i> I can only imagine the research that must have gone into this one. I am very impressed with how she put in all those details yet still managing to make all of it smooth enough, plausible enough to have the story simply flowing with nary a hitch. Then consider that all that’s been woven into this thrill filled mix of politics, journalism and zombies … and my brain would have/could have said ‘ouch,’ but not once did it do so. Instead, I was all gimme gimme gimme. <br /><br /><i>Characters.</i> They’re none them the ones I first loved in Feed or Deadline but it’s <b>Shaun</b> in particular who had me teary. All that pain and all that awareness, then all that resignation had me following him alongside his mini family. I like him; <i>I have this massive book crush on him.</i> The first books showed this none serious type with a suicidal tendency, events in those books simply stripped him down leaving him simply suicidal. And he knew it too, as did the team, YET they stuck to it nonetheless… yes, things got uncomfortable and twisted and sad, but sweet too. So much so that said mini-family thing was just one more thing in it that drew me in a little more. And let me not fail to add, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <b>Georgia.</b> There she was. Aware of who she was BUT also aware of who she wasn’t. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Mind bending and complicated? Why, yes. Yes, it was. <br /><br />Each of them saw things as they were; sure some were more suspicious than others but that each of them had their own things to deal with yet they stuck around and that fact positively warmed me. Don’t get me wrong this is all about the Masons’ but with the likes of Becks, Mahir and Alaric and Maggie, well, I love this book just a smidge more <i>because of them.</i> Add that all of them had done some sort of reevaluating about where they were and where they were headed with each of them coming to their own conclusions… so that you could smell the change coming. It just made things feel even more <i>charged.</i> See? This book…! <br /><br /><i>Sticky bits.</i> And all those questions and sticky what if’s and what about’s that I’d basically swept past in Feed and Deadline because <i>I</i> just couldn’t deal? Well, they’re dealt with here. And while initially jarring, (all those hints not withstanding,) I did <i>grasp</i> where they were coming from. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I even appreciate that it’s all eventually laid out for the world to see. Even if a tad unconventional, I might even holler out a <i>’Y ay!’</i> for them... because they deserve it, (given all the death and convoluted conspiracies, after all.)  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />While my favorite may be Feed that’s mainly because I’d never read anything quite like it before. Deadline and Blackout are tied in second because damn it… they both had me bawling like an idiot. Considering that all three are a bag of zombies, blogging and political what’s-it, well me bawling at all is quite a feat.<br /><br /><b>Love.love.love<br />5/5</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17166662644870441601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_344974840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/344974840" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/344974840?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=own-physical">own-physical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer344974840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12751122290001136877">4.5 stars.<br/><br/>"Sometimes the hardest thing about the truth is putting down the misassumptions, falsehoods, and half-truths that stand between it and you. Sometimes that's the last thing that anybody wants to do. And sometimes it's the only thing we <i>can</i> do." - Georgia Mason.<br/><br/>When I write book reviews, I usually save my recommendation of the book until the end. But <i>Blackout</i>, and the Newsflesh trilogy itself, should not be put off until the end. <i>Feed</i>, the first book in the series, is a novel that I woul</span>
  <span id="freeText12751122290001136877" style="display:none">4.5 stars.<br /><br />"Sometimes the hardest thing about the truth is putting down the misassumptions, falsehoods, and half-truths that stand between it and you. Sometimes that's the last thing that anybody wants to do. And sometimes it's the only thing we <i>can</i> do." - Georgia Mason.<br /><br />When I write book reviews, I usually save my recommendation of the book until the end. But <i>Blackout</i>, and the Newsflesh trilogy itself, should not be put off until the end. <i>Feed</i>, the first book in the series, is a novel that I would recommend to almost anybody - the book and the entire series encompasses zombies, blogging, politics, and a gamut of themes and morals. It's absolutely amazing. So, before I get into <i>Blackout</i>, I highly recommend that you check out <i>Feed</i>, if you haven't already. It will blow your mind.<br /><br />Mira Grant does not lose any steam in this final installment of the Newsflesh trilogy. She continues the story seamlessly from the chilling ending of <i>Deadline</i>, and grabs readers all the way to the gripping finale. Her thickening of the plot and her masterful use of foreshadowing via cleverly-dropped hints does not disappoint, and made it difficult for me to resist picking up the book even when I had work to do.<br /><br />Her characters were wonderful, as always. The reemergence of Georgia's cool and collected voice and the continuation of Shaun's somewhat crazy narration provided for an interesting mix, and the side characters were fleshed out to the point that I hesitate to even call them side characters. In most books there are certain characters who fade into tasteless black, but each of Grant's gave the story more spice and color.<br /><br />Like Georgia, Grant's attention to detail amazed me. All of the minute intricacies and convoluted plot complications were handled and resolved deftly. The chilling cloning procedures and revolutionary scientific aspects of the story were rendered plausible, which, while scary, made the book even more believable.<br /><br />The only reason I don't give <i>Blackout</i> a full five stars is because it did not entrench me as emotionally as I would have liked it to. I felt the tears approach once, but they did not actually arrive like they did when I read <i>Feed</i>. I did feel more of a connection than I did in <i>Deadline</i>, though, so it was a step-up from the second book in the series.<br /><br />Once again, if the plot summary of the first book sounds even barely intriguing to you, I highly recommend you pick it up. This series is different than anything I've read before. And, like the Masons did, I'm only telling you the truth.<br /><br />*review cross-posted from my blog, <a href="http://thequietvoice18.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">the quiet voice</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12751122290001136877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I liked her a lot. <br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I was really said when she decided to blow herself up to save them all T_T  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br> <blockquote>
  <b>
    <i> “Last guy I was interested in turned out to be an incestuous necrophiliac," she said. "So no, not currently dating, and definitely not doing any more shopping in the 'sociopath' category” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br>The ending was just what i wanted it to be. Concluded and explained. Thankfully they didn't bore with politics and they more focused on Kellis-Amberlee and other events. <br><br><b>spoilers for the end of the book</b> =&gt; <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ In the end, we conclude that government wanted to control people with a zombie apocalypse, through fear. And all because they couldn't find the cure. Even though they knew that people with Retinal KA were starting to get immune to virus, they killed them to hush that. It was really wrong and i am glad that that solved at the end. Also Shaun &amp; Georgia getting a happy end too. If only Mira Grant made another chapter with what they are doing, IT WOULD BE AMAZEBALLS  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>____________________________________________<br><br>● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ● ●<br>____________________________________________ <br><br><b> MY NEWSFLESH REVIEWS: </b><br><br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356844696" rel="nofollow noopener"> Feed (Newsflesh Trilogy, #1) </a> <br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356844805" rel="nofollow noopener"> Deadline (Newsflesh Trilogy, #2) </a> <br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356844836" rel="nofollow noopener"> Blackout (Newsflesh Trilogy, #3) </a> <br> ◉ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/405283655" rel="nofollow noopener"> How Green This Land, How Blue This Sea (Newsflesh Trilogy #3.5) </a> <br><br><b>

<div id="review_359361236" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/359361236" />
    <a title="Kara Babcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock"><img alt="Kara Babcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580776593p2/1188122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/359361236?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kara Babcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kara Babcock" href="/user/show/1188122-kara-babcock">Kara Babcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316081078" href="/book/show/13135026-blackout">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=hugo-nominee">hugo-nominee</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=2013-read">2013-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=post-apocalyptic">post-apocalyptic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1188122-kara-babcock?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer359361236&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer359361236" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13398665235696194058">So, here we are again. Mira Grant is back for one last kick at the Hugo novel can with <em>Blackout</em>, the last in her <em>Newsflesh</em> trilogy. All the mysteries are cleared up, all the questions answered. Georgia and Shaun Mason are reunited to kick zombie butt one last time and fight back against the government corruption that has put the entire world at risk. Sort of. I think.<br/><br/>Actually, this book is kind of a mess.<br/><br/>My opinion of the <em>Newsflesh</em> series has much in common with another very popular science-fict</span>
  <span id="freeText13398665235696194058" style="display:none">So, here we are again. Mira Grant is back for one last kick at the Hugo novel can with <em>Blackout</em>, the last in her <em>Newsflesh</em> trilogy. All the mysteries are cleared up, all the questions answered. Georgia and Shaun Mason are reunited to kick zombie butt one last time and fight back against the government corruption that has put the entire world at risk. Sort of. I think.<br><br>Actually, this book is kind of a mess.<br><br>My opinion of the <em>Newsflesh</em> series has much in common with another very popular science-fiction series, <em>The Hunger Games</em>: each successive book has been less satisfying and less coherent. There's no question that Grant is trying hard or that she has a story to tell, but what she has produced here leaves much to be desired.<br><br><strong>This book is far too long.</strong> This in and of itself is not a cardinal sin. You know what is? Making your book too long because you have pages upon pages where <strong>nothing is happening</strong>. I was lucky enough to read this in electronic form, albeit a PDF of a print version that was apparently 630 pages or so. It took me a few days, because I had trouble gaining traction. A book set in a post-apocalyptic zombie universe should be fast-paced and gripping. A political thriller about a conspiracy to conceal the truth behind a deadly pandemic should be tense. And the truly terrible thing about <em>Blackout</em> is that, for a few shining moments, it manages to be all these things. Grant demonstrates, sporadically, she is capable of the backstabbing betrayal, the cliffhanger smash cuts to another character, the heart-to-hearts prior to a massive sacrifice. But just when you think the book is finding its footing and about to really get started, it stumbles, and all the tension fizzles.<br><br>I'd be interested in seeing an inverted image of <em>Blackout</em>, a negative-space version of the book. That is, I'd like to see all the scenes that happen off the page and none of the scenes actually on the page. Because I kind of think the former might be more interesting than the latter. Case in point: towards the end of the book, we learn that the evil megalomaniacs at the CDC are keeping the President in check by holding his wife and kids hostage. But it's OK, because the Secret Service has a plan to rescue them when Georgia and Shaun are ready to help expose the conspiracy. George says, "Do it" and then marches off to confront the CDC baddie. A few pages later, the Secret Service reports that it has been done.<br><br>Earlier in the book, it takes hundreds of pages for the gang <em>not</em> to make it to Florida and capture some live mosquitoes for their resident mad scientist. We get pages upon pages of the gang driving their van through Seattle. When it comes time for them to break into the CDC building there, we don't see it. We see the ending and the aftermath, but the actual break-in is a handwave off the page. Similarly, when it comes time for the Secret Service to launch their rescue op, we see none of it, and it goes off without a hitch. Consequently, such an easy time of it made me feel like the resolution to <em>Blackout</em> was more contrived than it should have been. I'm not saying it <em>is</em> contrived, but that's what it feels like because of the way Grant handwaves what should, by all rights, be a conflict-ridden and difficult operation. (If it weren't, why did the Secret Service wait all this time to do it?)<br><br>This weird inversion of action and exposition is a problem throughout the book. There isn't much actual zombie combat in <em>Blackout</em>. We hear about a lot of zombie combat, but again, we don't get to see much of it. Instead, the characters prefer to spend their time chewing the scenery and yelling at each other. I've never seen such a dysfunctional group of people who nevertheless insist upon letting a crazy guy lead them and who only relieve him of the duty of command <em>after</em> the crazy guy's sister <em>actually</em> comes back from the dead to speak to him instead of just speaking to him as a voice in his head. If these are the people we need to depend upon to save the world, I am not optimistic for our chances.<br><br>The lack of zombie combat is problematic, because my appreciation of the Otherness of this world deteriorated as a result. Grant goes on <i>ad nauseum</i> about the necessity for blood tests and decontamination and how everyone lives in fear. Yet by not actually showing us much in the way of threatening zombies, she sends mixed messages. The constant threat of danger that was suspended over the characters' heads in <em>Feed</em> isn't present here. The world has become boring, a known quantity, and Grant does nothing to raise the stakes to change that. (Because those freakish mosquitoes that can act as a vector for the zombie virus? Yeah, that all happens <em>off page</em>.)<br><br>In fact, the more I pick at the 2041 of <em>Blackout</em>, the more problems I have with it. In my review of <em>Deadline</em>, I remarked that the series makes a mistake in "not going full cyberpunk". That is to say, Grant takes the importance of the Internet in a post-zombie apocalypse world as a given. She elevates and speciates bloggers, making them the heralds of the brave new world. Yet she never quite conveys how blogging has evolved in thirty years. I understand that, having suffered a zombie apocalypse, innovation might be slower and technology might not change as swiftly. But if human society has truly taken refuge online to the extent that bloggers are eclipsing old media, there should be social and mimetic differences from the Web of today.<br><br>This doesn't seem to be the case: Grant uses blogging as a plot device, with pithy epigraphs at the beginning of each chapter. She fails to explore how blogging as a medium has evolved--and I'm not talking about the new requirements to pass firearms tests to become a journalist. In the past few years, cyberpunk has emerged as somewhat of an unrealistic projection of where technology might take society. Regardless, what it did really well was envision a world so different that it is a little bit alien. <em>Blackout</em> doesn't achieve this. Instead, 2041 is unbelievably like 2013, but with zombies.<br><br>And we only really have Shaun and Georgia's word on all this. What I mean is, Grant remarkably restricts our exposure to different viewpoints in this new world. We never get a sense of the bigger picture; there is seldom a minor character who isn't mixed up in this conspiracy who can simply offer a slightly different perspective on events. Grant tells us (there's that verb again) time and again that bloggers in general, and Shaun and George's crew specifically, are influential. Apparently George is so important that the CDC simply <em>has</em> to bring her back from the dead with some incredibly expensive cloning. We are <em>told</em> that "the people" are outraged when the Masons expose some government wrongdoing or when Shaun and George make a broadcast from the battlefield. Somehow, though, amidst all these pages of nothing happening, Grant doesn't actually find time to show us any of this outrage.<br><br>It's such a shame, because this is not an awful series. There is a good story in here struggling to escape a very convoluted and often contrived plot that makes only a little more sense than the characters who ostensibly drive it. <em>Blackout</em> should have been the triumphant conclusion to a thrilling zombie trilogy. Instead, it seems to have inherited all of its predecessors' problems and very few of their strengths. The result is a book that doesn't deliver what it promises. Sadly, this is all too representative of the series as a whole, which dreams big but never quite manages to achieve the heights to which it aspires.<br><br><strong>My reviews of the <em>Newsflesh</em> trilogy:</strong><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190304549" rel="nofollow noopener">← <em>Deadline</em></a><br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons BY-NC License" width="88" height="31" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380238653i/681550.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13398665235696194058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_213803938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/213803938" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4844873-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299680097p2/4844873.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213803938?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/4844873-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4844873-laura?shelf=zombie">zombie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213803938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13075725350892953861">The End. *sigh*<br/><br/>I held on, savored, and remained in Mira Grant’s Blackout as long as I possibly could. Stretched out the love and experience for weeks. But I guess I had to hit the last page eventually. Leaving the book home for the first time the other day, hell—made me feel naked! And left a true ache in my heart. <br/><br/>Blackout is filled with government conspiracies, mad scientists, zombie bits, running, and the ever present battle for the truth. If you have read Feed and Deadline, then I have no do</span>
  <span id="freeText13075725350892953861" style="display:none">The End. *sigh*<br /><br />I held on, savored, and remained in Mira Grant’s Blackout as long as I possibly could. Stretched out the love and experience for weeks. But I guess I had to hit the last page eventually. Leaving the book home for the first time the other day, hell—made me feel naked! And left a true ache in my heart. <br /><br />Blackout is filled with government conspiracies, mad scientists, zombie bits, running, and the ever present battle for the truth. If you have read Feed and Deadline, then I have no doubt this book will end up on your to-read list and pile very soon. If you have not started this trilogy yet--go, <b>GO</b> now! :)<br /><br />These characters—George, Shaun, Maggie, Becks, Mahir, Buffy, Aleric, and more—made a home in my heart and soul. Grant’s world is unforgettable. Her words have made me think, left me in awe, cry like a baby, and laugh out loud. A small smile and smirk crosses my face whenever I think of Shaun. Crazy, cute, loveable, did I say crazy already?—Shaun! Like an old friend who stole a piece of my heart, I believe Shaun and the whole gang will always make me smile. I miss them all already. <br /><br />One of my favorite aspects of this book and series is the fact that the dead are never forgotten. Some for obvious zombie reasons. ;) But the dead shouldn’t be. Sometimes in life, people try to ignore the pain and grief by closing up or not talking about loved ones they have lost. But I felt this series, Blackout especially, showed how individuals can impact and influence our lives in good and bad ways long after they are gone. The dead do live on in our hearts and minds to inspire and influence. Of course, the issue of grieving and letting go is a whole other sad, haunted tale and message. A sad message that broke my heart, but also one I took to heart. <br /><br />These pages also inspired me to crack open my first can of Coke in a very looooong time! I buckled! The temptation was too much. It really is like liquid crack! :D<br /><br />So….Cans in the air….To the end. To the truth. To life, love &amp; death. Whether you poke things with a stick or type away behind a screen—hope you find what makes your heart happy!<br /><br />Cheers! :)<br /><br />*******************************************************************<br /><br />Who said that? :D<br /><br />What? I pulled a lot of quotes. Hehe…Had to do something with them. :) Not everyone is included in the game. It does not mean a thing. These quotes are not spoilery. They really just reveal my twisted sense of humor. Hehe…Have fun!<br /><br />Who said this?<br /><br />1.<br />“The scary part, the really scary part, the legitimately terrifying part, or the part that makes suicide sound like an awesome way to spend an evening?” <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Maggie! :D <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />2.<br /><b>“Less talky more shooty”</b><br /><br /><b>“Looks like we’ve both been having an interesting time. I’ll see your luxury hotel, and raise you one zombie bear.”</b><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The man stole my heart way back in Feed and squeezed the hell out of it in Deadline. Shaun “poke it with a stick” Mason! I just adore the man. Want to keep him safe and warm. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />3.<br />“Yes, because allowing the crazy people to set the rules is absolutely always the way to ensure one’s survival in a hostile situation.”<br /><br />“Well, that’s that, then. We’re all going to die. Charming.”<br /><br />“I haven’t been ‘cool’ since arriving in this godforsaken hellhole you persist in claiming is a civilized nation. I am, however, ready to go violate a few more laws.”<br /><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Mahir. That one was easy. His voice is so distinct and hilarious mixed in with everyone else’s style. I love Shaun and Mahir’s chemistry. They always made my heart smile and ache. :) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />4.<br /><b>”Is there a reason we keep winding up in places that should have stayed in their horror movies?”</b><br /><br /><b>”So no, not currently dating, and definitely not doing any more shopping in the ‘sociopath’ category.”</b><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ That would be my woman, Becks! She can go toe to toe with the best of them. Hell, most of the time the men hide behind her! :) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />Just a little fun to hide my pain and grief. I need a re-read of this series ASAP! :)<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_331222198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/331222198" />
    <a title="Jammies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/193219-jammies"><img alt="Jammies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298671765p2/193219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/331222198?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jammies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jammies" href="/user/show/193219-jammies">Jammies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316081078" href="/book/show/13135026-blackout">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/193219-jammies?shelf=brainsssssssss">brainsssssssss</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331222198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6535447500407294308">Why have I spent every free minute of the last four days reading the same book twice? Because it's a sci-fi / horror / political thriller / love story / action movie novel all rolled into one, and it is intense.<br/><br/>Okay, thing one about this review--spoilers. I'm not going to put them in individual spoiler tags, I'm going to spoil-code everything after thing two and if you keep reading, on your head be it.<br/><br/>Thing two--you need to read these books in order. If you haven't read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7094569.Feed' title='Feed by Mira Grant' rel='nofollow noopener'>Feed</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8429687.Deadline__Newsflesh_Trilogy___2_' title='Deadline (Newsflesh Trilogy, #2) by Mira Grant' rel='nofollow noopener'>Deadline</a>, stop r</span>
  <span id="freeText6535447500407294308" style="display:none">Why have I spent every free minute of the last four days reading the same book twice? Because it's a sci-fi / horror / political thriller / love story / action movie novel all rolled into one, and it is intense.<br /><br />Okay, thing one about this review--spoilers. I'm not going to put them in individual spoiler tags, I'm going to spoil-code everything after thing two and if you keep reading, on your head be it.<br /><br />Thing two--you need to read these books in order. If you haven't read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7094569.Feed" title="Feed by Mira Grant" rel="nofollow noopener">Feed</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8429687.Deadline__Newsflesh_Trilogy___2_" title="Deadline (Newsflesh Trilogy, #2) by Mira Grant" rel="nofollow noopener">Deadline</a>, stop reading this review, get your butt to the library or bookstore and read them. Come back here in a week, when you've read both books and then had a meal, a shower and some sleep. Trust me, you will only take time away from these books with great reluctance.<br /><br />Spoilers ahoy! <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[First of all, as good as both <i>Feed</i> and <i>Deadline</i> were, Blackout surpasses them. Almost all of the characters are fully realized human beings, whether they're natually born or cloned. I cried when Maggie left her dogs, when Georgia and Shaun were reunited, when Becks died, when Mahir held his daughter. <br /><br />Second, the action was adrenaline-inducing, top-notch and perfectly paced. The moments of calm throughout the novel gave me a chance to catch my breath, which I needed almost as much as the characters.<br /><br />Third, Ms. Grant's science is completely believable, clearly well-researched and yet does not wind up being either dry or dumbed-down. <br /><br />Fourth, there are delicate little love notes to geekery scattered throughout the book.<br /><br />Fifth, the humor in this book is intensely funny, just as black and twisted as I was raised to enjoy, and it rang true to the characters and the situations.<br /><br />Sixth, the surprises, oh my dog, the surprises! I had no idea about Shaun and George being a couple. They hid it from the world and Ms. Grant hid it from the reader for two and a half books! On my second reading of Blackout, I found a clue or two, but I sure didn't notice them the first time. And the fact that the CDC managed to take the President's wife and children hostage was another shocker.<br /><br />Seventh, the number of quotable lines is driving me crazy. I need someone else to read this so I can share quotes. If I say "No more Mr. Nice, Heavily Armed, Pissed-Off Journalist" here in my blog, it doesn't mean a thing. Or "Listen to the dead girl." I could go on and on and on and on...<br /><br />Eighth and finally, the language is impeccable. Yes, I found a whole six typographical errors, I can't help it, I have a typesetter's brain. However, I found nothing wrong in the entire book in terms of word choice, order or rhythm. Ms. Grant wrote 632 pages full of lightning rather than lightning bugs, and I am thankful that she was willing to share them with us, and thankful that Orbit helped her do so. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />P.S. I bought an inscribed copy from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.borderlandsbooks.com">www.borderlandsbooks.com</a> and Ms. Grant drew a chainsaw after her name--how cool is that?</span>
  <a data-text-id="6535447500407294308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_469803759" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/469803759" />
    <a title="Lori Redman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295078-lori-redman"><img alt="Lori Redman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316193975p2/5295078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/469803759?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lori Redman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lori Redman" href="/user/show/5295078-lori-redman">Lori Redman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=trilogy">trilogy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=e-book">e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=zombie">zombie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5295078-lori-redman?shelf=just-plain-awful">just-plain-awful</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer469803759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4729770373646491837">This entire review contains spoilers. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[<br/><br/>Shaun (with an insane Georgia in his head) and Georgia (who is now a clone resurrected from the dead) are back at it again! Fighting zombies! Drinking coca-cola! Dabbling in politics! Briefly mourning all their friends who are dying for them! ... Um, great. <br/><br/>Seriously, though. Shaun is now immune to the disease. He is the ONLY KNOWN PERSON who is immune to the zombie disease, and it's because "he was close to Georgia". What, is that something s</span></span>
  <span id="freeText4729770373646491837" style="display:none">This entire review contains spoilers. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br /><br />Shaun (with an insane Georgia in his head) and Georgia (who is now a clone resurrected from the dead) are back at it again! Fighting zombies! Drinking coca-cola! Dabbling in politics! Briefly mourning all their friends who are dying for them! ... Um, great. <br /><br />Seriously, though. Shaun is now immune to the disease. He is the ONLY KNOWN PERSON who is immune to the zombie disease, and it's because "he was close to Georgia". What, is that something sexual that I have to read between the lines for? Or are you implying that by breathing the same air as Georgia he is now suddenly somehow immune? Science doesn't matter in this book, as long as you have an over-active imagination and no desire to learn FACTS. Which these main characters claim is the most important thing ever. Too bad we don't get any in this book. Once again, Shaun mentioned several times: "They started to explain the science behind it, but I wasn't listening." Really, Mira Grant? Really? That's the best you can do?<br /><br />Also, Georgia is a test subject, whom the government spent billions of dollars on trying to correctly clone. Her other clones (numbering in the thousands or millions) have been terminated for not being a perfect match that they can manipulate. Georgia is the rebel- she's fierce and just like her original self! (My voice, I must add, is dripping with sarcasm and agony). But wait, even though there are super evil doctors working on her, she enjoys killing them all because her good friend Rick ordered for her to be cloned. But Rick is spared a bullet at the end, because he's her friend. Right. Why was this girl so important for the government to spend billions of dollars on her cloning process? So that she can tell the truth, of course! Wait, what? Can't Rick do that? Rick, the new VP of the country, who was also previously a blogger, can't crack down this information, upload it to the Internet, and report it? Nope- we have to spend BILLIONS OF DOLLARS so that the poor, poor souls who enjoy reading this drivel can have their heroine back. Because seriously, the author wanted that happy ending and by golly she was gonna get it. <br /><br />This book is filled with more sacrifices of secondary characters- one of whom being the girl that Shaun slept with in the last book- for which Shaun and Georgia offer some sarcastic "we're tired of writing eulogies, they're dead" comment on their blog. It's pretty much how Mira Grant handles all secondary characters- they are all disposable, except for Shaun and Georgia. I mean, Georgia 2.0. "The living dead girl" as she calls herself. I'd like to point out an interesting difference here. George R.R. Martin has a habit of killing of his characters, but their deaths mean something. You know, like, it adds to a plot point. Mira Grant kills off characters because, when all else fails, the book needs zombies and government conspiracies. And where there are zombies and government conspiracies, there should also be death. Preferably the death of side characters, because Mira Grant is WAY too into the whole necrophiliac incestuous relationship going on to kill off Shaun and Georgia 2.0.<br /><br />Oh, the plot. It hurts my head. There isn't a cure, but we can adapt, but the government doesn't want us to adapt, so the government kills people with reservoir conditions, but doesn't tell people about there not being a cure because they want people to hope, but meanwhile they are busy killing all the people, because the virus can be mutated in a lab with genetically created mosquitoes, so that the government can unleash them on the population to stop a blogger from posting on her blog. WHAT? Oh my goodness gracious. Can we please just have a zombie book that does NOT involve the President, the Head of the CDC and some RIDICULOUS conspiracy theory? This was so half-assed with plot holes as big as the sun that it's not even worth thinking about. <br /><br />Oh, and everyone's totally cool with the whole incest thing. It's like, not even an issue. Let's just make out in front of everyone. OH. MY. GOOD. GRACIOUS. "There's only one way for me to truly know that you, a clone, are my sister" "But Shaun- We've never-" "Please, you must. I need to know" . UGH. For all their endless supplies of ammo and weaponry, I hope they saved enough for everyone in the audience to be put out of their misery.<br /><br />The President, having signed death warrants, approvals for the Southeast to be wiped out by the plague that he created, and funded unethical scientific research, is EXONERATED and SEEN AS A HERO- because his family was being held hostage and he was scared. Oh, boo-hoo. I guess no one cares that you killed thousands of other families out there, as long as you did it with good reason. No plot holes here, no sirree.<br /><br />That being said. The author writes a suspenseful story. When she's not busy repeating herself. My goodness, every few chapters she repeats the story thus far. And then repeats it from a different character's point of view. And then uses the SAME WORDS and phrases to say something. About 15 times. It's clear how she came up with her "brilliant alternate ending"- she just loves to copy/paste. <br /><br />Wait, was I trying to say something good about the book? Yes, that's right. This book gets 2 stars partially because I'm so glad it's finally over, but also partially because the book continued to keep me reading. I hated almost every second of it, but I still couldn't put it down. It was a compelling, if completely bat-shit-insane-full-of-plot-holes-and-extremely-poorly-written book.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />For what I consider to be a very thorough and fabulous review of this last book, please see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350104240" rel="nofollow noopener"> this review </a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4729770373646491837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
